Source: 2026-06-02 Understanding MCP: The Universal Bridge for AI Models | by MongoDB | Apr, 2026 | Medium
Type: Pasted Text
WebSync metadata title: Understanding MCP: The Universal Bridge for AI Models | by MongoDB | Apr, 2026 | Medium url: https://medium.com/@MongoDB/understanding-mcp-the-universal-bridge-for-ai-models-8ff8b4392e21 date: 2026-06-02T19:42:21.750Z parsing method: defuddle Sitemap This article was written by Farhan Hasin Chowdhury. Part 2 of 3 Discover how the Model Context Protocol transforms AI-tool integration from a fragmented landscape of custom solutions into a unified, standardized approach. Learn the three-layer architecture, communication patterns, and security model that power connections between AI assistants and external data sources like MongoDB. In Part 1 of this series, you learned how to integrate MongoDB’s MCP server with popular clients like Claude Desktop, VS Code, and Cursor. You configured connection strings, tested queries, and experienced firsthand how natural language can interact with your database. But how does this all work under the hood? What makes it possible for different AI applications to communicate with different data sources using a unified approach? The Model Context Protocol (MCP) is the answer. Understanding MCP’s architecture isn’t just academic knowledge — it’s practical insight that helps you make better integration decisions, debug issues when they arise, and even build custom MCP servers when existing ones don’t meet your needs. This article takes you deeper into MCP itself. You’ll learn about the protocol’s origins and the problem it was designed to solve. You’ll explore the three-layer architecture that separates concerns between hosts, clients, and servers. You’ll understand how JSON-RPC messages flow between components and how different transport mechanisms work for local and remote servers. You’ll also learn about MCP’s three core primitives — resources, tools, and prompts — and how they enable different types of AI-data interactions. By the end of this article, you’ll have a solid mental model of how MCP works. This understanding will serve you well whether you’re troubleshooting a connection issue, evaluating which MCP servers to use, or planning to build your own. The protocol’s design decisions will make sense, and you’ll appreciate why certain patterns exist. Whether you completed Part 1 or are jumping in here with some MCP experience, this article assumes familiarity with basic client-server architecture and JSON. If you’ve worked with REST APIs or similar web technologies, the concepts will feel familiar, just applied in a new context. The Problem MCP Solves Before MCP existed, connecting AI models to external tools and data sources was a fragmented experience. Every AI platform had its own way of handling external integrations, and developers found themselves building custom solutions for each combination of AI tool and data source. Consider the landscape before MCP. OpenAI introduced function calling, which lets GPT models invoke predefined functions. But this approach was OpenAI-specific. If you built integrations for ChatGPT, they wouldn’t work with Claude or other models. LangChain emerged as a popular framework with its own tool abstraction, but again, tools built for LangChain didn’t automatically work elsewhere. Developers created custom API wrappers for specific applications, but these were application-specific and couldn’t be reused. This fragmentation created what’s known as the N×M problem. If you have N different AI models and M different tools or data sources, you theoretically need N×M custom integrations to connect everything. Want to connect five AI platforms to 10 different data sources? That’s 50 different integrations to build and maintain. Each integration has its own authentication patterns, message formats, error handling approaches, and documentation. Figure 1: The N×M problem before MCP, where each AI model required custom integrations with each tool, creating a complex web of connections. The solution MCP provides is elegant. Instead of building custom integrations between every AI model and every tool, you build one MCP client implementation for each AI model and one MCP server implementation for each tool. The protocol itself handles the communication between them. This reduces the integration burden from N×M to N+M implementations. What was needed was a universal protocol that any AI model could use to discover and invoke any tool. This protocol needed standardized patterns for authentication, capability discovery, and message exchange. It needed to work across different transport mechanisms — from local process communication to remote network connections. And it needed to be simple enough that developers could implement it without extensive overhead. MCP delivers on these requirements. It provides a standard way for AI applications to discover what tools are available, understand how to use them, and invoke them safely. The protocol handles the plumbing, so developers can focus on building useful tools and integrations. MCP Architecture Deep Dive MCP uses a three-layer architecture that cleanly separates responsibilities. Understanding these layers helps you see where different concerns live and how they interact. The MCP host is the AI application you interact with directly. Claude Desktop, VS Code with GitHub Copilot, Cursor, and similar tools are all MCP hosts. The host handles the user interface, manages conversations with the AI model, and coordinates with MCP servers. When you type a question about your MongoDB data, the host receives that input and orchestrates everything that follows. The MCP client is the protocol handler embedded within the host. While users often refer to applications like Claude Desktop as “MCP clients,” technically, the client is the component inside the host that manages MCP communication. The client handles protocol-level concerns: establishing connections to servers, exchanging capability information, formatting requests, and parsing responses. Most users never interact with the client directly — it works behind the scenes. The MCP server is where the actual tools and data live. MongoDB’s MCP server, GitHub’s MCP server, file system servers, and similar components are all MCP servers. Each server exposes specific capabilities — tools that can be invoked, resources that can be read, prompts that can be used. Servers are designed to be lightweight and focused on a single domain. Figure 2: The three-layer MCP architecture showing how hosts contain clients that manage connections to multiple servers, each providing access to different external resources. The interaction flow works like this. When you ask an AI assistant to query your MongoDB database, the request goes to the host. The host’s embedded client establishes a connection to the MongoDB MCP Server (if not already connected). The client and server exchange capability information so each knows what the other supports. When the AI decides to use a MongoDB tool, the client sends a tool invocation request to the server. The server executes the operation against MongoDB and returns results. The client passes these results back to the host, which incorporates them into the AI’s response. Several key architectural decisions make this work well. Servers are designed to be lightweight and single-purpose. A MongoDB server handles MongoDB; a GitHub server handles GitHub. This separation keeps servers simple and maintainable. Clients handle protocol complexity, shielding servers from having to implement intricate communication logic. Hosts manage the user-facing aspects — conversation flow, AI model integration, and presentation of results. This separation of concerns means you can update or replace any layer independently. A new MCP server can work with existing hosts. A host can add support for new transport mechanisms without affecting servers. The protocol specification provides the contract that keeps everything compatible. Communication Protocols and Transports All MCP communication uses JSON-RPC 2.0 as its message format. If you’ve worked with JSON-RPC before, MCP will feel familiar. If not, the format is straightforward: structured JSON messages with specific fields for requests, responses, and notifications. A JSON-RPC request includes a method name, optional parameters, and a unique ID that correlates the request with its response: The corresponding response includes the same ID and either a result or an error: JSON-RPC also supports notifications — messages that don’t expect a response. These are useful for events or status updates where acknowledgment isn’t required. MCP defines two primary transport mechanisms for carrying these JSON-RPC messages: stdio and HTTP with server-sent events (SSE). stdio (standard input/output) is used for local servers. When an MCP host launches a local server like MongoDB’s, it communicates through the process’s standard input and output streams. The host writes JSON-RPC messages to the server’s stdin; the server writes responses to its stdout. This approach is simple, secure, and requires no network configuration. The host manages the server’s process lifecycle, starting it when needed and stopping it when done. HTTP with SSE is designed for remote servers. The client makes HTTP POST requests to send messages to the server. The server uses Server-Sent Events to push responses and notifications back to the client. This transport works across network boundaries, enabling MCP servers to run on remote machines or in cloud environments. The typical message flow during a session looks like this:
Initialize: Client sends an initialize request with its supported protocol version and capabilities
Capabilities exchange: Server responds with its capabilities and supported features
Tool discovery: Client requests the list of available tools via tools/list
Tool invocation: Client calls specific tools as needed using tools/call
Results: Server returns operation results Here’s what a tool invocation request looks like: The choice of stdio for local servers is intentional. It keeps everything on your machine with no network exposure. The host process directly controls the server process, making security and lifecycle management straightforward. For production deployments or shared infrastructure, HTTP with SSE provides the necessary network capabilities while maintaining the same message semantics. MCP Primitives: Resources, Tools, and Prompts MCP defines three core primitives that servers can expose: resources, tools, and prompts. Each serves a different purpose in AI-data interaction, and understanding when to use each helps you make the most of MCP integrations. Resources represent data that AI models can read. They’re identified by URIs and are read-only by design. Resources let AI models access information without performing actions that have side effects. Examples include file contents, database records, API responses, or configuration data. A MongoDB MCP server might expose collection schemas as resources. The AI can read mongodb://mydb/users/schema to understand the structure of the users collection without executing a query. Resources are particularly useful for context — helping the AI understand what data exists and how it’s organized. Resources use URI patterns for identification: Tools are actions the AI can invoke. Unlike resources, tools can have side effects — they modify data, trigger processes, or interact with external systems. Each tool has a name, description, and input schema that defines what parameters it accepts. MongoDB’s MCP server exposes several tools: find for querying documents, aggregate for running pipelines, insertMany for adding data, and others. When you ask Claude to “find all movies with rating above 8,” the AI uses the find tool to execute that query. Tool definitions include JSON Schema for parameters, enabling validation and helping AI models understand what arguments to provide: Prompts are reusable prompt templates exposed by servers. They allow servers to suggest specific interaction patterns or workflows. A prompt might guide the AI to analyze data in a particular way or follow a specific diagnostic procedure. For example, a MongoDB server could expose an “analyze-collection-structure” prompt that guides the AI through examining a collection’s schema, identifying patterns, and suggesting optimizations. Prompts can accept dynamic arguments for customization. Here’s how these primitives compare: In practice with MongoDB’s MCP server, you’ll primarily interact with tools. The find, aggregate, list-databases, and collection-schema operations are all tools. When you ask an AI assistant to explore your database or run a query, it invokes these tools behind the scenes. The distinction between resources and tools matters for security and predictability. Reading a resource is always safe — it won’t modify anything. Invoking a tool might change data, so hosts typically prompt for confirmation before executing tools with side effects. Capability Negotiation and Discovery Before an MCP client and server can work together, they need to understand each other’s capabilities. This happens through an initialization handshake that establishes what features both sides support. When a client connects to a server, it sends an initialize request containing its protocol version and the capabilities it supports: The server responds with its own capabilities and protocol version: After initialization, clients discover available tools through the tools/list method. The response includes every tool the server exposes, along with descriptions and input schemas: This returns a complete catalog of tools. AI models use the descriptions to decide which tool to invoke for a given task. Good descriptions are crucial — they help the AI understand when each tool is appropriate. Similarly, resources/list returns available resources, and resources/templates provides URI patterns for dynamic resources. A template like mongodb://{database}/{collection} tells the client it can construct URIs for any database and collection combination. This capability negotiation enables several important behaviors. AI models can adapt to server capabilities — if a server doesn’t support certain operations, the AI won’t try to use them. Servers can evolve over time, adding new tools or resources, and clients automatically discover these additions. When a capability isn’t supported, the system degrades gracefully rather than failing unexpectedly. The protocol version negotiation ensures compatibility. If a client and server support different protocol versions, they can agree on a common version they both understand. This forward and backward compatibility means you can upgrade clients and servers independently without breaking existing integrations. Security Model and Trust Boundaries MCP’s security model is built around clear trust boundaries. Understanding who trusts whom helps you reason about security implications and implement appropriate safeguards. The trust architecture follows a chain. Users trust the host application — you choose to install and use Claude Desktop or VS Code, and you trust them to behave appropriately. Hosts trust the servers they’re configured to use — you explicitly configure which MCP servers a host should connect to. Servers don’t inherently trust each other — they operate independently without assuming anything about other servers. Figure 3: MCP trust boundaries showing the chain of trust from user to host to servers, with servers isolated from each other. Authentication patterns differ between local and remote servers. Local servers using stdio transport rely on process isolation. The host launches the server process directly, and communication happens through stdin/stdout without any network exposure. The operating system provides isolation between processes. Remote servers using HTTP/SSE transport need explicit authentication. This might involve OAuth flows, API keys, or custom authentication mechanisms. The server validates credentials before accepting connections, and the transport layer can use TLS for encryption. MCP includes a permission model for tool execution. Tools can be marked as requiring user confirmation before execution. Hosts should prompt users before invoking tools that modify data or have other significant side effects. This creates a human-in-the-loop checkpoint for potentially dangerous operations. The distinction between read and write operations is important. Read-only operations (like querying data) are generally safer than write operations (like inserting or deleting documents). MongoDB’s MCP server supports a -readOnly flag that disables all write operations, providing an additional safety layer for exploration and analysis. For MongoDB-specific security, several considerations apply. Connection strings contain credentials and should never be committed to version control. Use environment variables or secure credential storage instead. Database users should have appropriate permissions — create a dedicated user with minimal necessary privileges rather than using an admin account. Network-level access control (IP whitelisting in MongoDB Atlas, firewall rules for self-hosted) adds another layer of protection. Sensitive data handling requires attention. AI models may see query results, so consider what data the MCP server can access. Using read-only mode and limiting database permissions helps contain what information the AI can retrieve. For production environments with sensitive data, evaluate whether MCP access is appropriate and what guardrails should be in place. MCP Ecosystem and Adoption MCP originated from Anthropic, the company behind Claude. Anthropic open-sourced the protocol, making it available for the broader community to implement and extend. This open approach has driven adoption across the AI tooling landscape. The current MCP ecosystem includes several major adopters. Claude Desktop has native MCP support since Anthropic created both Claude and MCP. VS Code integrated MCP through GitHub Copilot, bringing the protocol to millions of developers using Microsoft’s editor. Cursor, the AI-first code editor, built MCP support into its platform. Windsurf, Continue, and other AI-powered development tools have followed. The server ecosystem continues to grow. Official servers exist for popular services: MongoDB, GitHub, Slack, Google Drive, and others. These servers are maintained by the service providers themselves, ensuring compatibility with their APIs. Community-built servers cover additional use cases — databases, APIs, developer tools, and specialized domains. The open specification means anyone can build an MCP server for their particular needs. Server examples from the ecosystem include:
Databases: MongoDB, PostgreSQL, SQLite.
Version control: GitHub, GitLab.
Communication: Slack, Discord.
Cloud storage: Google Drive, Dropbox.
Developer tools: Docker, Kubernetes, AWS. Looking at future directions, several developments are likely. Standardization efforts may emerge as more organizations adopt MCP. Enhanced security features could address enterprise requirements for audit logging, fine-grained permissions, and compliance. Multi-server orchestration — coordinating actions across multiple MCP servers — represents an area for protocol evolution. The protocol’s design accommodates future expansion. New primitives could be added alongside resources, tools, and prompts. Additional transport mechanisms could support different deployment scenarios. The capability negotiation system means new features can be introduced without breaking existing implementations. Building Mental Models for MCP Analogies help when learning new protocols. Two comparisons are particularly useful for understanding MCP’s role and design. Analogy 1: USB for AI Before USB, every hardware peripheral had its own connector type and protocol. Printers, mice, keyboards, and storage devices all required different cables and drivers. USB standardized hardware connections — any USB device works with any USB port, and the operating system handles the details. MCP does the same for AI-tool connections. Before MCP, every AI model had its own way of connecting to external tools. MCP provides a standard interface: Any MCP server works with any MCP-compatible host. You can plug MongoDB’s MCP server into Claude Desktop, VS Code, Cursor, or any other host, just like plugging a USB device into any USB port. Analogy 2: REST for AI tools REST standardized how web services expose APIs. Instead of proprietary protocols, services provide predictable HTTP endpoints with standard methods (GET, POST, etc.) and self-describing responses. Clients can discover and interact with REST APIs using common patterns. MCP standardizes how AI tools expose their capabilities. Servers provide discoverable interfaces through the tools/list and resources/list methods. Input schemas describe what each tool expects. The protocol defines standard patterns for invocation and response handling. Several mental shifts help when thinking about MCP:
From “function calling” to “tool invocation”: MCP tools are more general than functions. They include descriptions that help AI models understand context and appropriate usage.
From “API integration” to “capability exposure”: MCP servers don’t just wrap APIs — they expose capabilities in a way AI models can discover and reason about.
From “hardcoded connections” to “dynamic discovery”: Instead of building specific integrations, you configure which servers to use, and the discovery mechanism handles the rest. When should you use MCP? Consider these scenarios: AI-assisted workflows where you want natural language access to tools and data; multi-tool orchestration where actions span multiple services; context-aware applications where AI needs to understand and work with external data sources. MCP shines when you want AI models to interact with external systems in a standardized, discoverable way. Conclusion MCP solves a real fragmentation problem in AI integrations. Instead of building custom connections between every AI model and every tool, the protocol provides a standard interface that reduces complexity from N×M to N+M implementations. The architecture separates concerns effectively. Hosts handle user interaction and AI model communication. Clients manage protocol-level details. Servers expose tools, resources, and prompts for specific domains. This separation keeps each layer focused and maintainable. The three primitives — resources, tools, and prompts — provide flexibility for different interaction patterns. Resources offer read-only data access. Tools enable actions with side effects. Prompts guide specific workflows. Together, they cover the common ways AI models need to interact with external systems. Security is built into the design through clear trust boundaries, capability negotiation, and support for authentication patterns. The protocol supports both local (stdio) and remote (HTTP/SSE) deployments with appropriate security mechanisms for each. Key takeaways from this exploration:
MCP is a protocol, not a product — it’s designed for interoperability across different AI platforms and tools.
Understanding the architecture helps with debugging and optimization when issues arise.
The capability discovery system enables AI models to adapt to available tools dynamically.
Security considerations should guide how you configure and deploy MCP servers. In Part 3 of this series, you’ll move from understanding to implementation. You’ll explore advanced MongoDB MCP configurations, build custom workflows, work with write operations, and learn production deployment strategies. The conceptual foundation from this article will help you make informed decisions as you build more sophisticated MCP integrations. For now, revisit your MCP setup from Part 1 with fresh eyes. You now understand what happens when you configure a connection string, why tools appear in the list, and how messages flow between your AI assistant and MongoDB. Additional Resources
MCP Specification: the official protocol documentation with complete details on message formats, transport mechanisms, and primitives ,- Anthropic’s MCP announcement: the original blog post explaining MCP’s design rationale and goals
JSON-RPC 2.0 specification: the underlying message format used by MCP
MongoDB MCP Server source code: reference implementation showing how MCP servers work in practice MongoDB unleashes the power of software and data for innovators everywhere.

This paper presents the case of ChatTEDU, a dual-LLM architecture designed to pr...Abstract:Open access chatbots face escalating cybersecurity risks due to adversarial exploitation. This paper presents the case of ChatTEDU, a dual-LLM architecture designed to protect open-access AI systems from sophisticated adversarial attacks while maintaining the user experience quality. During a two-month deployment at TED University, we analyzed 4501 unique real-world interactions, including 180 malicious attempts targeting the system through prompt injection, jailbreaking, and content manipulation attacks. Our dual-layer security approach separates content moderation from response generation by using two specialized language models. The first model (LLM-1) acts as a security filter, analyzing incoming queries for threats, whereas the second model (LLM-2) generates educational responses only after validation. This architecture successfully blocked 100% of the identified attacks, with only 0.28% false positives, demonstrating robust protection without compromising legitimate educational interactions. The system handles over 100 concurrent users during peak registration periods without security breaches or performance degradation. Attack analysis of the case revealed that 77.8% of the threats used technical exploitation rather than content-based manipulation, with multilingual attacks comprising 15% of the attempts. The dual-LLM approach introduced only 18% latency overhead, while providing comprehensive protection against prompt injection, jailbreaking, spam insertion, and denial-of-service attacks. This study provides practical guidance for implementing robust security measures in public-faced AI deployments worldwide.The Dual-LLM Security Architecture of ChatTEDU employs two sequential large language models: a security layer (LLM-1) filters user queries for safety, and a response laye...Published in: IEEE Access  ( Volume: 13)Page(s):  183156 - 183170Electronic ISSN: 2169-3536DOI: 10.1109/ACCESS.2025.3623268Funding Agency:
